RATING: 8.2
All kinds of awesome. There are so many different unit types and technologies and stuff, it's nearly overwhelming when you are playing for the first few times. You could do or build something different every game for quite a few games and still learn about the way different units interact and how well some units/technologies are in different situations. (though it seems that archons, battlecruisers, and ultralisks are the way to go most of the time)

The combat deck ROCKS. It's like a whole game of DOMINION thrown into a conquest-type game, only better, because instead of buying a card that lets you draw an extra card or that is a useless VP, you get a Nuke card, or a Stim Packs tech that beefs up your marines, or Increased Carrier Capacity so everyone fears your floating beehive of doom. The cards have a Magic-esque feel to them, too, how they each have an attack and health value and sometimes special abilities, or some of them are enchantments/support cards that boost your main dudes.
At first, I felt that you drew way too many combat cards during the game, but after a couple plays I saw that that isn't true at all; I only had a ton of combat cards when I was researching and not fighting. The rest of the time, you'll be attacking people and getting attacked and you'll lose your Ultralisk because you don't have a matching card or stuff like that.
Speaking of losing your Ultralisk, the cards do a good job of emulating unit health/shield levels, too. Let's say you have in your hand, 2 Ultralisk cards (one with health 9 and one with health 7) and a zergling card. Your Ultralisk gets attacked and you use your health 9 card to destroy some Terran meat. Later that turn, you get attacked again and use your health 7 card, but your Ultralisk just barely manages to survive the battle. If you are attacked again before you get another Ultralisk card, that 'lisk is probably dead. The cards are great at representing health levels AND energy levels (for special abilities). This is why the Zerg get to draw a card after each battle; it represents their quick natural healing. The Protoss draw extra cards when they are attacked as a representation of their shields.

The order placement part of the game is cool, though it is probably the most difficult rule for people to grasp at first. It lets people see where people are doing things at the start of the turn, but they don't know what they're doing or when they are doing them. You can even mess up other people's plans by placing your orders on other people's. Also, the way you spend resources is simple and intuitive and is great because it doesn't require bookkeeping. On the other hand, managing all your little worker tokens is annoying and can get "fiddly".

Unfortunately, all players need to be paying attention and trying to prevent everyone else from winning for the game to be tense and exciting to the end. The only thing I don't like about the game is the way the special victories can end it in a spectacularly anticlimactic fashion sometimes. (I'd like to try Brood War to see how it changes things, or try out Brood War's 'survival' variant win conditions)

One of the most fun things about the game is all the times you can quote the units from the PC game. You get to say stuff like ""My life for Aiur!!!" and "I'm about to drop the hammer, and dispense some indiscriminate justice!" One more good thing about the game is that it feels and plays like an epic "monster game", but you can fit it into a few hours.

Also, the minis simply rock; they have got to be the most awesome board game components I own. The only thing that keeps it from being played more is the high level of thinkyness it requires (and the accompanying mental exhaustion).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Starcraft is a solid game.  An FFG production with excellent components, it looks stellar on the table.  The order placement mechanic from "A Game of Thrones" is in place here, whereby you place orders in stacks and then resolve them top down, so you kind of have to think in reverse order, which is a little weird at first but later grows on you.

Combat is resolved through unit skirmish matchups and card plays, modified by keywords.  In practice, this works out very well and introduces a hand management aspect into the game.

In most of the games I've played, each player feels like they're still "in it to win it" until the last game turn.  You're always "this close" to having victory yourself when someone else pulls out the win.  This is largely due to special victory conditions, which flavor each faction differently.

The game is highly thematic, moderately complex, takes a while to play, has little downtime (mostly when other players are in combat), takes a ton of table space, is kind of lengthy to teach, and has excellent reference material.  It's a fun, interesting, and engrossing game that plays best with 4 or less, but can support up to 6 (which increases the table space required, the potential downtime, and game length).

RATING: 5.5
Not a game I would own because like others of its ilk, I'd never get it to the table. But surprisingly, it's rather easy to understand if explained by an experienced player (the rules are far too intimidating otherwise). It has some whacky mechanics - e.g. the reverse action selection and the odd impact of units that are worthless without correspondingly great cards. It gives you some of the theme of the computer game and a bit of the flavor - but not the zest and excitement. It drags along too - over staying its welcome with more then 3-players. All in all, it's a fun time with loads of glitter and a big production - like an overproduced MGM musical.

RATING: 4
2008-08-05
This is a rather long game with soooo many rules that everytime you're in a mood to play StarCraft you have to read the rules booklet again.

2016-03-01
I would use StarCraft: The Board Game as a study case how (not) to translate a computer game into a board game. On PC you have the CPU to do all the upkeep and bookkeeping for you while it also enforces the rules. When you play unplugged, you have to do all that stuff by yourself. And this is the greatest design flaw in this boardgame. 

StarCraft throws the players into a quagmire of rules, components and administrative tasks that have no end. 90% of the table time is spent on something else than playing, and of the remaining 10% majority is downtime (divide 10 by the number of players to get the approximate figure). 

This game relies only and solely on the shoulders of the most devoted fanboys who do not mind spending hours just reorganising fiddlybits over and over again. I love the computer game but most likely will not play this ever again, for it has one of the worst decisions to playtime ratios.

RATING: 7.6
Actually a very good game. Not too long, the victory conditions are achievable and the bits are the most detailed I've ever seen.
It is very aggressive and you are almost obliged to attack every turn, but apart from that it has a very modern euro feel about it. Particularly good is the way the combat decks work - you have a standard deck you draw cards from during combat, but buying technology adds cards to the mix, so there is an element of deck management to the game.
The races are quite different and the game is over in 2 hours, nicely done.

RATING: 6
A good game but not for me.  The theme is excellent and surprisingly it accurately reflects the computer game.  I like the unit building and resource management.  The alternate (special) win-cons are a nice touch, though they don't appear to be entirely balanced.

The game I played took forever.  6 people, 6  hours, and we didn't even finish.  Granted, it was a learning game, but the rules are [i]very[/i] dense and often difficult to decipher.  Not for the faint of heart.  Also not really for young players.  Rounds take a long time with 6 people since you go around 4 times placing orders and 4 more times executing them.

Combat is okay.  It was done well, and is very true to the computer game, but it felt slow and somewhat tedious.

A good game, just too long and too complicated for my tastes.  I'm not sure I'll play this again, though I recommend others give it at least 1 try, particularly if you're a fan of the computer game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
FAVOURITE GAME - It is an awesome game of space domination based on the Starcraft universe. You control either Terran, Zerg, or Protoss armies (there are two of each available) trying either to gather victory points or either trying t fulfill army specific vicroty conditions. Each turn starts by planning the actions, placing a number of order tokens into stacks on each planet, with orders placed later obstructing the ones that were placed before them. This allows players to set up combos of their own, but also to obstruct plans of other players. Then everyone has the chance to execute one order available at a time. The game has excellent quality miniatures of units known from the video game.

RATING: 6.3
1 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
[i]Posted: 4/2/13, Rated: 9[/i]

Originally released in 1998 (!!!), [i][thing=70722][/thing][/i] has arguably become one of the most recognizable, and the most successful video games ever.  It was the perfect blend of sci-fi geekdom: [i]Aliens[/i], [i]Predator[/i], and [i]Starship Troopers[/i] all rolled into one cohesive experience.  Nine years later, a board game version of this classic was released.  This development is not surprising: there was the [thing=7479]mildly disappointing board game adaptation[/thing] of [i][thing=68458]Warcraft III[/thing][/i], and [thing=17223]the surprisingly good adaptation[/thing] of [i][thing=69309][/thing][/i].  How would Fantasy Flight fare this time around?

A disclaimer before I continue: I heart all things Blizzard.  My friends and I played the hell out of [i][thing=70722][/thing][/i].  If you calculate "enjoyment" of a game by dividing its purchase price by the number of times you play it, then the per-play cost of [i][thing=70722][/thing][/i] can be measured in pennies.  Maybe even in fractions of a penny.  And I purchased multiple copies of the game.  I tell you all of this to let you know that I wanted to like this board game.

The packaging alone is epic.  It is the classic [family=9983]Fantasy Flight tombstone size[/family].  These boxes have to be seen to be believed.  They portend endless amounts of chits and figurines, with playing times to match.  One minor nitpick that I have with these games is that there is so much material inside of the box, that it is nearly impossible to keep everything inside without discarding the insert.  Don't worry, dear reader: my OCD was and is equal to this task, but it takes time to carefully repack the box after each play.

You should be careful with this game, anyway.  [i]StarCraft: The Board Game[/i] ([i]SCTBG[/i]) comes with exquisitely molded figurines for each of the three races in the video game: Terrans, Protoss, and Zerg.  Flying units are mounted on clear plastic bases.  These acrylic bases can snap at the joint where the unit figurine is mounted.  A couple of my units were broken in this fashion when I first unboxed them.  Fantasy Flight sent replacements, but care needs to be taken when returning them to the box.

The map in [i]SCTBG[/i] works well.  Planets are placed on the board and then connected via linking routes.  Players will only be able to move (attack) along these routes.  [i]SCTBG[/i] takes an interesting diversion from the video game with the inclusion of "Z-routes:" in an attempt to model three-dimensional space, there are links which allow planets on opposite sides of the map to be connected by a single link.  In other words, players should try to imagine this map folding on top of itself to establish these extra routes.  I like this addition, as it makes it more difficult for a player to "turtle" in the corner of a map.  Not that turtling is a desired tactic in this game...

For the real goal is to secure and retain control points.  To do this usually requires you to attack other planets, and then to defend your gains.  You will also have to harvest Crystals and Vespene Gas.  It is possible through force mining to deplete the available resources on a planet, so you will need more.  Attacking and expanding require the use of Command Counters, which are the heart of this game.

In an attempt to model a Real Time Strategy game, [i]SCTBG[/i] provides each player with Command Counters: chits which allow the player to perform standard tasks like attacking, moving, research, etc.  In turn order, players place one of their command counters on the map, either on a planet they control, or on a planet connected to one they control (connected by one of those links).  Once all of these command counters have been placed, they are resolved in turn order provided a counter of the current player is visible.  This means that the sequence of these counters is vitally important to executing your plans.  If you want to attack and then occupy a planet, then you must place the "occupy" counter first.  Some time later, you will place the "attack" token on the same planet.  When those counters are evaluated from the top down, then "attack" will be handled first, and "occupy" second.

This mechanic takes a few attempts to appreciate.  Once you have grasped its significance, it is divine.  You can try to interrupt other players' plans by placing your own tokens to impede their progress.  You can place a token as a diversion.  You can mobilize your troops and summon overwhelming forces.  These nuances do a great job of replicating the tension found in the video game when making decisions surrounded by the fog of war.

Beyond this core mechanic, [i]SCTBG[/i] has all of the high production value chrome that you would expect from Fantasy Flight.  Every faction has its own deck of cards, which are used for upgrades and higher-level units.  There is definitely a "tech tree" represented by the building upgrades which are tracked on player sheets.  Battles feel epic with front line and supporting units.  All of these items will feel nostalgically welcome to players of the video game.

[i]StarCraft: The Board Game[/i] does an excellent job of capturing the core decision making and resource management of its progenitor.  The game feels epic in scale.  As a bonus, it doesn't take as long to play as other members in the series of Fantasy Flight tombstone boxes -- if you don't count the amount of time spent packing all of these components back in the box.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
This is a great game.  I quite like it, when there are four or more players.  Is it's an entirely adversarial game, players are encouraged to battle to prevent others from winning.  At times this means that in three-player games king making becomes a problem, which our group likes to avoid.  These teamups can change as the game progresses, but this is not a playstyle we tend to enjoy.  With more than three players, I feel this game really comes into it's own and moves from 'ok' to 'good'.  I'm not terribly fond of the 'special victory conditions', and the intricacies of combat take some practice and thought to understand well, but it's a game well worth playing in a group of 4+.  

I've bumped my score on this game.  Most excellent strategy game, great interaction!  The kingmaking is not as pronounced as I'd first feared, and the multiple paths to victory can still give a player being teamed-up on an opportunity to win the game.  Excellent design!

The fiddliness of the game is irksome.  Lots of set up time and teardown time, and the gameplay is not the most intuitive.  I'm not giving up on it, but it might take some time to hit the table again.  Rating dropped to 7.

RATING: 7

The game is much shorter than you think it is going to be (roughly 5 turns, with the first whole turn just branching out) and there is a lot of combat.  The way the board is constructed makes you have to get into each others business by turn 2 and attacking is so much better than defending that the game becomes more about just kicking each other in the shins the best you can.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I've accepted the fact that I'm predominately a "Eurogamer." However, every once in a while you just want to Blow. Stuff. Up.

Starcraft was my "riskiest" purchase. It's fairly expensive, it's chock full of components, it's a "coffin box" Fantasy Flight game... things that are not typically Euro. But, I was intrigued by its "hybrid" mechanics described in various reviews, and I was looking for a lengthier game, so I took the plunge.

After one play, I feel pretty good with this purchase. It took a good 45 minutes to setup and provide a rules overview, and 3 more hours to play it. But myself and three other friends felt that this deserves another play. I was afraid that this would be too clunky, too burdened by excess rules, but for our first play, the time invested returned a surprising amount of fun. We all expect it to flow much more smoothly with familiarity.

This is an aggressive game. A number of rules encourage offense, and there's no hiding from others. However, the game offers some intriguing choices in the range of units and the balance between "teching up" and keeping a strong presence. The stacking of orders is very clever and mentally challening. I was a bit concerned that each player's "combat deck" would feel too random or forced, but it actually worked very well. Plus, every battle is divided into separate "skirmishes," and this approach emphasizes the different abilities of different units. Combat feels meaningful and strategic.

The components and miniatures are impressive. The rulebook, while incredibly detailed, is also very clear and very well organized. Kudos to the rules writers for clarity and consistent terminology. 

We're just scratching the surface with this game. I'm not sure if this will stay a "9" rating, but right now, I'm eagerly awaiting the next chance to play.

Update: 2nd play, and I'm dropping this to an 8, with hopes that it rises again. I got my arse handed to me. Wow, this is a brutal game. Early blunders can really cost you the game. Given the complexity of all the rules, it's hard to play this game infrequently--it demands to be played with enough regularity to keep the basic rules in your head so that you can focus on the strategy. The good news is that my fellow gamer friends are willing to put in the effort, so hopefully this hits the table soon. I think there's a really good game in here... it just simply demands attention and TLC.

Update: third play... OK, now THAT'S more like it! Played a great game with four players that ended after reaching the the tail end of the "game clock" event deck. I really want to play this again soon. The tech tree and diversity of units, the combat decks, the anti-turtling aspects of the game... this just offers a lot of fun and justifies the longer playing time.

Update. Traded this away after playing forbidden stars a handful of times. There is enough overlap that I didn't need to win both, and forbidden stars is more likely to hit the table.

RATING: 8
Initial impressions after two fumbling learning sessions and two actual GAMES (all 2-player)... I think I'm going to like this one. A lot. Visually beautiful, interesting gameplay, lots of choices, a good amount of depth. The various factions are well-defined and well-designed, with key differences built in via individual mechanics, abilities, special victory conditions, etc. The biggest drawback was a less-than-optimal rulebook layout (probably exacerbated by my utter unfamiliarity with the video game... or ANY video game, for that matter). Initially I found myself frantically flipping pages whenever I came across a new or undefined term or phrase (often NOT listed in the index). I finally just sat down and read the rules from cover to cover, like a novel, jotting down questions as I went and assuming they'd be answered eventually (they usually were). I then repeated the process. Twice. I'll likely do it again before the next game, too. A little study seems a small price to pay for what promises to be a very rewarding gaming experience in the long run... AND it has generated a huge amount of interest among many friends who have NEVER expressed any interest in participating in "game night" before.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
A great sci-fi game. I love StarCraft in PC, but please don't really compare it to the boardgame version. It has different feeling and mechanism, it's a brave move from FFG and I think it's a good move. Rather than try to be exact like the PC game as possible, they made a good playable space wargame. There is development and research, battle of armies, and area controlling. And the components are pretty.

When you get familiar with the faction's ability and mechanics, this become quite cool subgame of cunning play. The only downside is all of them are geared towards military (It's Starcraft after all).

RATING: 9
StarCraft feels heavy but plays relatively quick, probably because it cuts to the chase and gets players into the action almost from the get-go.  The learning curve is a little steep, but the rules are intuitive and once you have these down, you're in for some glorious carnage.  While there are a wealth of options available to expand your armies, there just isn't enough time to flesh them out fully, so the challenge is working out just how much you need to develop your forces to eke out a win.

RATING: 8
12/24/07: I just got this for Christmas! Can't wait to get this on the table! Will rate upon first play.

OK, with two plays under my belt, I feel this is a solid "8" for me. I'd actually rate it higher, but I was Protoss-Tassadar both games, and I feel the Protoss are a difficult race to play, so that weighs in my mind a bit (having said that, I did not show poorly in either game, but didn't win either). This should get a good number of plays because it is pretty straight forward. One minor note of caution - despite what the "normal" play time for this game is supposed to be, expect it to be significantly longer for your first play. Both of my first two games (a four player and a five player) took SIX hours to complete. I expect that to come down, but I can't see a four or five player game going much under three hours in any circumstance.

RATING: 8.5
Most strategy games I have played tend to favor the defender, but this game turns the roles around, making it a game filled with action. Next to the 3 different races, there are many different styles of play, giving this a very high replay value.

The downside of the game is that it takes me about 1 hour to explain to new players. Once you've played it, though, the rules are clear. It also takes some time to play and the game map can get very large. There is also a very large set of components that you need to find a way to store as unfortunately the default box doesn't provide a proper way to store them. Unfortunately in my case, two of the (flying) miniatures arrived broken in the box.

In the end though, this game is excellent!

RATING: 7
Relatively fun Ameritrash, and fairly faithful to the computer games. It's essentially a pure warfare version of [i]Twilight Imperium III[/i], using a somewhat annoying ordered action placement mechanic instead of any sort of "role" mechanism, and removes the political element. The upshot is a game that still has quite a bit going on, but is shorter than TI3 by about a quarter. So, if you're looking for "TI3 light", this may be the game for you.

If you're unfamiliar with the computer game, however, I think you're going to have a much harder time enjoying this game. This is in part because of the difficulty distinguishing the units (the plastic bits fail here) and in part because it's going to be difficult to remember which units can do air combat, land combat, or both.

one of my favorite games. Action packed and incredibly balanced. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
 I finally got to try this game. 5 players, and it was the first time for all of us. Is the hype really worth it?  Yes, and no. It's not a bad game - in fact, I think it's very good - but there are some problems.  
 
The GOOD:  Lots of very cool pieces.  Well made boards. Lots of action choices for each turn, which maximizes re-playability.  For those familiar with the PC game, the game mechanics are well designed, and make sense.
  
The BAD:  With so many options per turn, AP can set in very quickly, especially for the less-experienced gamer.  Without some prior experience with the PC game, the decision trees are just overwhelming.  The playing time should be listed in days, not hours.  (It took 3 hours to get through three turns, and we were nowhere near done)  The down time when you're not involved in the action gets very tedious.  The rulebook could be laid out better, and some things were left out, or not explained in enough detail.
  
The VERDICT: A not-so-well designed game, that really can't be played in a regular evening gaming session.  One must be prepared to devote the better part of a day to play, and I, for the most part, just don't have the time. I'd play it again, but I'll never buy it.  Definitely not a game for rookie gamers.  I give it a '4', simply because it's so difficult to play.  It probably deserves an 8, except that I'll never suggest it.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
After the colossal failure that was the WoW game, I was dreading seeing how badly they would mess up Starcraft.  But man is this good.  It isn't a literal translation, but it captures the spirit of the RTS in a way that WoW perfectly whiffed.

The order token mechanic is how you need to keep several plans going in your head at the same time.  Resource management is, of course, resource management.  The method of using workers as you pay allows resource raids.  While the map looks like planets, it is really just an RTS island map (although without navy).  The attacker picking the matchups perfectly matches how you can scout the other guy and then give perfect attack commands while he just sees your guys coming and can pretty much just area guard.  The card fighting system is a bit of a stretch, but you could think of it as different player skill in using each unit.  But while the hand management system can't be fanwanked, it is just awesome so it can be forgiven.

RATING: 6
Only one game and a ton more to explore.... players play a race of humans, bugs or ... something else.  You begin on connected planets and must colonise, exploit colony resources to build stuff (units, buildings, technology) that will allow you to stomp your neighbours.  Very tight on board situation means you're throwing down in combat very quickly.  Combat is a nice system of skirmishes, where every skirmish is a card play.  Sounds clunky but worked well.  I liked this game - it felt well developed and tight, with a well written rulebook.  Minis and planets look fantastic, the chits and cards are busy and functional if you know where to look.  Good game, quite quick once you've played once or twice.  Rating could easily climb - unlikely to drop.

RATING: 6
Falls somewhere between Nexus Ops (great) and Twilight Imperium 3 (not so good).   For two or three players, it's a good choice, incorporating so much of the computer game buildings and units.   The overall feel of gathering resources and building up units and technology is well done.   The coolest innovation is the tech decks which add cards into your main combat deck.   It's like a little mini CCG within the game.   Combat is very much like Magic The Gathering, and the area control of resources feels like TI3.   But offense is encouraged so the game moves along nicely.  It's a decent implementation and an engaging game for a couple hours.

RATING: 8
A true Fantasy Flight big box game, but one that remains reasonable. Long play, but not so bad you really want to quit before the game ends. Doesn't have the complexity or fiddliness of Twilight Imperium (3rd. Ed.) Though there are still 40 pages of rules, they are much easier to comprehend and remember during play because they just make more sense to begin with. 

This game does a great job of recreating the feel of the computer game. Capture planets to gain more resources to create more and better units with which to go kick somebody's ass. I've read that the rules for this game promote conflict and don't reward turtling as much as some other games, but that was not my experience. The player that won our game was the one that turtled the most and had the most secure location.

Had a great time, though. Will play again.

RATING: 6.8

NOTE - do not play with more than 4 players will take too long.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
** All plays of mine are using the Brood War expansion. However, this comment is strictly for the base game. 

So far it's one of my favorite games. Lots of fighting, lots of awesome minis and lots more strategy than I thought there would be.
The boys at work ate this game alive. Honestly it was all they could talk about for 3 days, and we played almost one full game a day. No problems at all! 

Pros:

- Miniatures: are awesome and lend themselves to modding some Starcraft "mini" games. Also, while they are made of a sort of "rubbery" plastic, I like this because they don't feel brittle. They also have a good amount of detail. While not perfect and amazing, they are pretty and really bring the theme to bare. 

- Order Tokens: are awesome, and lend half the strategy to the game. You can mess yourself up badly if your not careful, but you can do the same to others as well. Bluffing and thinking ahead will reward players a lot.

- Card based combat: Not nearly as luck based as I thought. While it is ultimately luck of the draw, there was only 2 battles I remember that I lost due to lack of good cards. There is a lot you can do here to ensure you have the right cards for the fight.

- Technology Cards: Really make the game feel like the PC game, but without making it the same experience. You have a lot of play on units, and some units are better vs others, and weak vs others. You have to balance your forces much like the PC game, and those who utilize their technology well, will have a huge advantage.

- Deck Building: was a bonus treat! I didn't realize that you built up your combat deck with technology until I read through the rules. Most tech you buy, you have to shuffle it into your combat deck. Meaning, your deck gets bigger, but may make good cards harder to come by. I found this was an awesome way to keep the game balanced.

- Multiple Victory paths: were also a little unexpected. While the capture-and-hold system and event cards were pretty interesting ways to end the game, the special victories that each race can have is awesome, and really makes the game interesting 

Cons:

-Initial learning curve: is a bit steep, not because the game it's self is complex (it's actually pretty simple), it's all the little bits and intricacies of the game is a little overwhelming to teach. Minerals, workers, 3 different decks in your play area, plus Event and Leadership decks as well. some 12 different units per race, race powers, order tokens, resource cards, unit limits, combat explanation, Conquest Points, transports, depletion tokens, and so on... It is harder to teach the game than to learn it. Well worth the effort though.

-Set up time: is long. After about 10-12 times of playing the game, all the players who've played before can help set the game up, which takes set-up time from about 45 minutes, to about 20 minutes. Still a bit more set up time than many would like, but it's well worth it.

- Planet connectors: are annoying. They stay gripped nicely through out the game and work great. When I take the game down though, I feel like I'm going to rip the printing off the planet when I undo the connection piece. While I don't know how else they would have done this, it feels a bit fragile and will eventually make some really big wear marks. 

Overall rating: 9

RATING: 5
Rating based on a single play in suboptimal conditions, so take it with a grain of salt. This game reaches new heights as of components quality. I've never seen such great miniatures. Also, the box is packed with card, counters, and great board elements. This constrasts with the game itself. The gameplay does nothing to bring back the frenzy strategic feeling of the computer game. It has an "order system" similar to the one in "A Game of Thrones" to which was added a clever "stacking" twist that may create strange and interesting situations. Still, it's a little puzzly and can slow the game down quite a bit. Sometime it will also create the opportunity for blocking moves that have nothing to with the theme but are a side effect of the mechanics which leave a sour taste. However, it's nothing compared to the combats. This is the painful part. The combats simply break the game flow and increases downtime tenfolds. Still, even with these flaws, this would qualify as an OK Fantasy Flight title. What really kills Starcraft is its repetitiveness. You build units, expand your base, draw cards, attack, again, and again, and again. The strategical aspects are there, but they are buried under this heap of repetitiveness.

RATING: 6
played with 6 people and it had to much downtime. Seems a bit bad balanced (victory conditions...)

played it again with 2. Didn't change the rating.
On paper the game has very much I like about games.
- assymetric player powers: Not only that each faction has a special ability and a special victory condition but you also have the 3 races which play quite differently
- Deck building: I very much like the way you build up your combat deck and how it influences your combat abilities.
- Interesting combat: Its not only roll X dice and count the 6es (nothng against this but it doesn't give you any decisions). The combat System in Starcraft gives you many decisions and adds some kind of hand management.
- preplanned hidden orders: I like the way you have to plan your move ahead especially with the stacks which allow you to react to opponents plans and kind of block them.

BUT: After each game I had the feeling the game lasted to long for what it offers. When I want to summarize what happend in a game I can describe it in a few sentences but each sentence conects to 1h of gameplay. Perhaps some of the problem is that I never played with only experienced players. But I never had the feeling that we played very slowly.

RATING: 8
An innovative take on the AT style of games.  The design could have easily been a standard "dudes on a map chucking dice", but this works so much better.  The production is fantastic.  The game looks amazing when it is all set up in play.  The orders system works really well and provides a lot of tactical flexibility and tension.  The card combat works better than dice and allows for some deck building elements.  Best of all, it plays fairly quickly for this style of game.  Maybe a bit too quickly as it is sometimes difficult to get the big units built in time for the end.  I'm not a huge fan of the license (I can't tell a Protoss from a Cylon), but I like this game a lot.

RATING: 8.08

Too bad is so rare and expensive. If you know somebody that have it, you must try at least once. It lasts a few hours, but it doesn't feel like this. You'll not regret, guaranteed.

RATING: 7
I really like the mechanism of placing orders (mostly in reverse order) and being able to cover other peoples orders to mess them up.  The special victory conditions are interesting and give each person a different objective.

What I don't like is the very lopsided benefit to attacking, the attacker gets to pick pretty much everything and gets more cards.  Almost never will a defender win a fight even if the two forces are relatively balanced.  There is also a huge benefit to familiarity with the pieces and thier abilities which no newbie will have.

So you probably will not like your first few games but (which is unfortunate when a game can take 6 hours) but it does have lots of potential.

RATING: 9
I think this captures the spirit of the PC game quite well! It even manages to make it turn-based..with a pseudo real-time mechanic. The components are amazing. It is a very tight game at times. Quicker than Twilight Imperium. Plays 2-6. It's 90% conquest though. You must enjoy attacking other players.

The order tile laying mechanic is unique...and if other players can guess what you are going to do on their planet you can get screwed pretty easily. You literally stack your orders..and then they are carried out in reverse order. (Top to bottom.) So, imagine order A is to move to/invade a planet. The other player can put an order on top of your order A and that order would be carried out first. He could effectively send more defensive units there making your invade order less effective.

The card combat is a nice feature as well. It makes you decide whether to hold your higher value cards for more important battles or play them for nearly guaranteed victories.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is a great strategy war game that is quite the beast of a game. Once you play a round I think it falls into place. I really enjoy the difference in play styles, the deck management, the combat, and the balance between building an economy and expanding. Mechanics place an extra incentive on playing aggressively which makes for a lot of interaction between players and keeps the game fun. Little to no down time keeps the game engaging even when it is not your turn.

It feels though that the end comes too quickly as you really don’t have time to really develop the higher tier units in the game, if you’re not very focused. I think this game becomes even better with the expansion.

I think it plays good with any number of players, even if I prefer to play it with 4 or 5 players. With fewer players you don’t have breathing room at all as there is a much smaller galaxy (it scales though). With 6 players the game takes longer time to finish.

RATING: N/A
One play with three and one with five. I think the game has a lot of good core mechanisms, particularly the order token mechanism. The level of detail that is laid on top of those core mechanisms is significant, however. Playing the game is not hard at all; getting the rules right in all the marginal situations requires rather a bit more work; playing the game [i]well[/i] would require dedication, I think. How well do you know your units, your combat deck, your tech deck...and those of your oponents?

No doubt there are lots of people for whom this sort of thing has a lot of appeal, people who want to be immersed in a particular game and to be able to pit their knowledge of its arcana against that of their opponents. I'm somewhat less enthusiastic about it, though; I prefer games where the complexity and interest comes from the nature of the rules, not the number of them.

RATING: 6
I believe there is a good game in there, and while the rules are quite complex, they are manageable and intuitive for the most part. What really complicates things is all the interactions between the many different cards and units. It is all very thematic, but if you are unfamiliar with the computer game (or haven't played it in many years, like me), that can be quite overwhelming. 

The diceless combat system is nice, but it is a little convolute and does take long as the players evaluate all their options. There are some production design issues, such as the planet areas being too small for the units, bases and all the relevant info. Resource cards cannot be stacked, as essential info is on the top AND bottom of them, and that just adds to the required table space, which would already be huge anyway. 

Also, there is some unnecessary chrome, with the event cards and special orders, which could have been left out completely. With a more streamlined, elegant design, this would be a much better game. As it is, the only things that make me keep it are the theme and fantastic miniatures.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a very good game, and while it looks overwhelming when you pull it out of the box, it really isn't as complicated as it first seems.  The game fills a great niche between Nexus Ops and Twilight Imperium III.

It is a grand space military empire game, with some civ elements and clever resource/action point management mechanics.  My three favorite things about the game:1. Insane production.  The miniatures and chits are incredible.  The cards and the board are beautiful.  The miniatures are well-sculpted and surprisingly easy to identify.  I cannot think of any other game I have ever played where the components and production even come close to this one.  It makes heroscape, heroquest, and descent look like the mayfair version of modern art or Frag by steve jackson.  The components in this game rank up there with "games" where the components are the game - like Warhammer or Hordes.  2.  Super replayability - six races with variable player powers, different victory conditions, modular board, and multiple different strategies to take with each race - exploration, war, high technology defensive strategies, plus multiple different strategies to take with how you develop your military and economy.  Tons of interaction always ups the replayability of a game.3. Tense decisions.  The secret action chit-laying mechanism really makes for some interesting decisions.  Its hard to plan your turn and you really feel like you accomplished something good when a clever plan goes off and you take someone off guard by invading their home planet when it didn't look possible at the beginning of the turn.  

My three biggest complaints:  1.   Initial planet grabbing is totally random.  In a game where 15 or 20 victory points can win you the game, starting with a 2 vp planet vs. a 1 vp planet (which scores each turn)is a huge advantage.    This could be house ruled easily with some sort of worker bidding for better starting planets.  2.   Combat is clunky, predictable and unsatisfying.  The play-a-card-with-your-unit-on-it is too anti-climactic.  Its not hard to rifle through your combat deck a couple of times with research and the three cards you draw for attacking.  Typically, its easy to tell whether you will win or lose a combat before it starts.  For a game where the combat is the meat and potatos you are building towards for most of the game, this crappy system is a real disappointment.  The tech system was done so well with the buildings, modules, and tech decks, yet the combat is totally boring "play your best card" and compare predictable numbers?  

3.  The ending is abrupt, too damn fast and anti-climactic!  Most of the special victory conditions aren't that hard to get, and once you do the rest of the players play beat-down-the-leader.  On top of that, if someone starts out with a 2 VP planet and manages to grab a couple other conquest point planets, they will hit 15 fast.  You have to watch out for this, or they will have the game in the bag quickly.  I wish all the planets were 1 vp/turn to eliminate this.  Maybe with more plays or more players this problem would be gone.  If not it would be easy to house rule.  Lets face it, this game should end with a massive fucking battle where plastic clashes with plastic in interplanetary destruction!  It didn't end that way for us.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
I thought this was going to be a game that I just HAD to buy at some point, but I was wrong. That's not to say it's a bad game, it just covers a lot of ground that has already been covered. Despite the massive amounts of units, each figure, be it a ship, alien, or mech, has nothing to set it apart from any other unit. If you have a card that matches it, the bigger units do more damage. If you don't, a little armadillo with spikes does as much damage as an enormous starship. The combat system was on the level of Risk. The real shining point was the little tokens that you place to execute orders. That aspect was well-crafted and added tons of strategy, while at the same time eliminating a lot of downtime. I'd rather play Risk 2210 for grand scale warfare, and I'd almost always take Nexus Ops over this game for superior combat, better victory conditions, and length of time. I would definitely play this again, but there is no way I would buy it considering I already own Nexus Ops and 2210. The $80 just isn't worth it, even for the cool order resolution system. All that said, there's nothing really BAD about it, it just didn't seem all that groundbreaking.

RATING: 7
It has taken several years to get this to the table, and it was a disaster when it arrived. Like TI3, there are many, many rules and bits. There are some new, unique mechanisms, with some borrowed. I'm not sure I like the rock-paper-scissors/A Game of Thrones style combat, as it seems like it is forcing it upon the game. This is StarCraft! Better technologies means better capabilities, more lethality, and more carnage, but it is not that straightforward. Instead, it is a matter of drawing the right cards for the right units. There is a strict limit on number of units on each space, which seems counterintuitive. The "first in, last out" order token mechanism is novel, but again, this seems forced on. In fact, most mechanisms about this game seem forced on, like it is alien and uncomfortable. I expected the streamlined battle carnage excitement of Nexus Ops, just on a bigger scale, just like the computer game--you know, amass resources, amass units, and then send them in for a cataclysmic climax. Instead, it is a cerebral exercise of trying to optimize [b][i]to[/i][/b] the mechanics, and not to the game. You lost me right there. Maybe I'll try this again on another rainy day, but it will be awhile before I revisit this. It is an unstable '7', waiting to drop at the first sign of more frustration.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
My impression is that some of the complexity is unnecessary (lots of unit types, each battle is broken into multiple skirmishes, lots of special combat rules, three card decks: combat, technology and events to get used to, technology by both cards and buildings plus other stuff). This leads to a lot of down time even with only 3 players. 

There are a number of interesting mechanics: Order tokens (a bit like Game of Thrones), Technology cards that you buy and then shuffle into your combat deck, Resources you mine by placing Workers on them during the turn and then (if you still control the area) you return them to your available workers area at the end of the turn.

The Starcraft bits are pretty (better than TI3) but most of the icons on the counters, cards and play mats are not obvious and some information is printed in places where it is hard to access when needed. E.g. it is hard to see what column of costs and abilities matches which of your pieces.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
As a game for 6 players, it's a DISASTER.

Lasts far too long, takes far too much physical room, there are too many unit types, too many chits, too many card decks, too much administration, not enough fun.

You need two tables to play this monster, incredibly good eyesight as you need to tell whether the inch-high unit 6 feet away is a marine or a firebat, an ability to memorise 20  shapes and the unit stats that go with them.

Combat is far too complex for the level of the game - can you attack land AND air units, are you in an area with a base and do you have air defences, what bonuses do the cards give, what is your effective strength and health, is there a valid supporting card, do you draw a card from your combat deck instead, do you have any technologies that alter the unit strengths, or event cards in effect, is your unit assisting or supporting, where can you retreat to, where do you find the space to set up the skirmishes that make up the battle, when and how does splash damage take effect - even when the unit retreated instead of being destroyed - the list seems to go on and on.  Something akin to the Nexus Ops combat would be more in line with the rest of the game.

The planet resource cards can't be stacked (that's if you want to know which planet they are for as well as their resource values), and you can't fit the bigger units on the board, and you have to cover up the resource values on the board with bases and units - so nobody can tell what they are.The rules are poorly laid out, so you spend large chunks of time trying to find out what rules apply, only to find that every rule has an exception that may only be mentioned on a faction's card - that you don't have - so nobody really knows what's going on until you've slowly run through all the options.  Once you have 3 or 4 technologies/events in play, you'll forget something vital until after the combat where it would have made a difference.  This is a computer game that should have stayed a computer game - there's just too much going on to allow you to enjoy the experience or immerse yourself in the gameplay. 

If you have 6 players and 4  hours, play Twilight Imperium III instead (although it did take 4 versions to get that game playable, so it may take a similar effort to make StarCraft a viable game for 6).

Do not play this game unless you enjoy endlessly moving cardboard and plastic around and mixing up your card decks constantly.

RATING: 7
Starcraft is a very thematic, but also plays well and even manages to avoid dice. It is a long game, but the outcome is rarely determined before the end as there are different ways to win and the event cards can provide surprises. Order placement and the card-driven combat are among the more interesting mechanisms in the game. The components are attractive, particularly the plastic miniatures. Unfortunately some of the the latter are difficult to identify, as the pictures on the player mats are very small. In terms of gameplay there a couple of drawbacks: that a player in a poor position can have little to do or even be forced to retire from the game, and that, with larger numbers of players, there can be fairly longs waits between turns especially when large battles take place.

Initial Rating: 6.5 (June 2012)

I’m a huge StarCraft fan, so when I played the game I was severely disappointed. It was too long for what it offered and I was bored at the end, too much downtime (the worst sin a game can commit). I will give it another chance soon as I bought the game, but I will probably sell it.

The game has great components and art. The minis are gorgeous and the board looks great. It’s been awhile since I last played the game AND perhaps the person who explained it made a lot of mistakes (thus I will play again soon), however I do remember that I didn’t like that you had so little defensive capability, but I liked the “deckbuilding” mechanic.

Bottom line, I will give the game another chance, however for this time/complexity ratio, I would play Twilight Imperium 3rd edition instead of StarCraft. I will comment and rate again after I play it again, for now I feel this is a 5.0 for me, a boring game that I would play from time to time due to its awesome theme.

Current Rating: 5.0

RATING: 4
Nexus Ops does everything Starcraft could ever hope to, but better and in a fraction of the time. Bad in its own right; atrocious when held to the standard set by the source material.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The most looked forward to game of the year. Did it meet the expectations? In terms of a board game, the [b]mechanics are solid. A little simpler than I thought[/b]. The biggest problem this game suffers from is the balance of units. For example: the unit tech tree for Terrans highly favors Starport compared to the Factory. Two of the Factory units can only attack ground, and the one that attacks air (Golaith - Tier 2) fights as well as a tier 1 Starport unit (Wraith). [b]While the unit variety works well in the computer game, it takes away from practicality in the board game[/b].

The system of [b]planning orders is quirky[/b] and takes time to get used to, and after players understand the mechanism, the play is somewhat linear. Your forced to attack and build in a predetermined order - otherwise you are set back for a turn. You could mess up someone's order queue, but 1) you need an adjacent base and 2) it will effectively negate the effect of your order - putting you at a disadvantage as well. Turrets are very annoying too, since it's a bit easy to crush a staging ground by attacking. And the retreat rules... take for example: if an assist unit is forced to retreat and makes it's way to an empty enemy base, it destroys the base at the end of the turn, even though it has no actual attack ability. Kinda anti-thematic. 

This game is definitely engaging as the battles go by quickly and attackers have a huge advantage, but it's so easy to effectively counterattack, especially with the protoss's 3 card draw for defense.

Lots of good ideas, but I was hoping for something a bit more of something... More strategy or tactics or unit abilities... [b]something feels missing[/b]. Maybe it's just missing the sound effects or music score.

Still an enjoyable strategy game, though not as complex as a wargame. Overall, I think the [b]mechanics look better on paper than in application[/b]. Too bad.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Based on the PC game of the same name, this boardgame takes a much higher view of the action. Instead of groundbased battle, player use their troops to fight for control of planets. The 3 races are well represented, with a huge library of technology and multiple ways to utilize their effectiveness. The best thing about this game is that it is geared towards attacking and not defense (unless you aim to win by Conquest Points). Fulfilling special victory conditions require players to go out there and take some hits to win the game.

The card-based combat system was NOT as elegant as I had read. Battles between 2 players was slow. Not as bad as MR or Age of Mythology (where the game effectively stops for the other players), but there was definite downtime.

The order system is unique and original, but doesn't really add much to the game, except forcing you to plan backwards (that just added downtime). After a few games, you do get used to working around the clunkiness of the system by holding back your important moves until you have last-player-advantage.

The Crystal/Vespan gas system was great. No need to collect a mountain of tokens. (i.e. Warcraft). Very elegant and well thought out.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Players start to lose lots of control over their ability to win once you get to 5+ players. In general, players only interact with 2 or 3 other players so a lot of an individual's success is determined by the skill of other players to stop those who you can't. This has a loose kingmaking feel to it and can become very frustrating when you win or lose not because of how well you played but how poorly others played. I would rate this game a 2 or 3 at 5+ players.

At 3-4 players this game is incredible. The intensity of players always being on the verge of winning and needing to be stopped is exhilarating. The constant shifting of power as players band together to stop the newest leader is excellent and has produced some of the most interesting games of anything I have ever played. At 3 or 4 players this would easily get a 10.

At 2 players it is far too easy for one player to pull ahead, at which point they are rewarded for their growth and become harder to stop. It is very risk in it's run away leader syndrome.

The bottom line is the game probably should have been limited to 3-4 player from the beginning.

RATING: 10
Currently my favorite game. It gets better (and deeper) every time I play it. Haven't found anything broken (other than a few battlecruiser stands, haha) after 20+ plays.

It's most fun with a large crowd, but it's a fully legitimate 2-player game as well, with no "neutral armies" (which is one of my beefs with Risk, Domaine, etc.).

It has a steep learning curve, and an even steeper "strategic understanding curve". It took me five or six plays to even develop a sense of how to plan a path to victory. If you invest the time to understand this game, it's a great one.

Addendum: I've played this about once a month for almost three years now, and I'm still not tired of it. If I could keep only one game, this would be it.

RATING: 6
[size=12][b]Top 200 board games list[/b][/size]

[size=12][b]Why I like StarCraft: The Board Game[/b][/size]
+ StarCraft: The Board Game seems to be the most thematic of all of the RTS board games.
+ I am a huge fan of the theme.
+ I liked most of the mechanics. 
+ There game requires meaningful decisions to win the game.
+ The complexity of the game convinced me to join a board game club and got me into board gaming.

[size=12][b]Fixable dislikes in regards to StarCraft: The Board Game[/b][/size]
/ The base game seemed flawed in a few regards. I hope to play the Brood War expansion to see if my issues are fixed instead of wasting my time writing them down. 
/ The rulebook is way too long. I suggest looking for a rules summary. 
/ The game set-up takes a long time. We are talking hours without some sort of way to organize the game.  The solution here was to establish a system. 
/ The miniature stands fall off. You need to super glue them together. 
/ There is some errata that requires fixing. 
/ The components list that comes with the rulebook is bad. 

[size=12][b]Why I dislike StarCraft: The Board Game[/b][/size]
- The game is hard to teach.
- The game drags on.
- I was not a fan of the deck-building mechanic in this game
- The game does not work with less than 4 players.
- The game drags on with greater than 4 players. 
- The combat has a few odd rules.
- The Typhoon promotion introduces turtling and I do not recommend buying this promotion. 

[size=12][b]VERDICT:[/b] Keep & Sometimes willing to play this game[/size]

RATING: 6.5
4+ hours is a really long time to sink into a game.  On top of that, the game is fairly dense with significant setup time and a 48 pages rulebook that put significant overhead whenever a new player learn to play.  The theme, units & mechanics are unpalatably geeky & technical.  If sure helps a lot if your players are into the eponymous video game lore.  There's significant downtime as other players take their turn and the flow is overall chuggy.  You really need the right specific group to make this enjoyable and you need to play semi regularly so that the rules are understood and setup expedited.  Mechanically, it's very solid and captures the theme right while creating real challenge and tension.

I don't feel the need to have my own copy of this game, and it probably isn't one I'd ask to play, but I'd still be more than happy to participate in future playings, particularly if everybody already had at least one game under their belts.

RATING: 6

The problem with all out war games is they are so dependent on the personalities of the other players.  Sometimes the worst in people comes out in such stressful do or die war games.  Also you have the issue of two players taking out one player and there is little you can do about it.

Other than the issues of multiplayer war games in general, this game has a lot of strategy involved.  Placing the 4 order tokens on the board is really tricky.  There is a lot of stuff you can build so what to build next is key.

The 'board' is so claustrophobic.  Everyone is pretty much right next door to you.  There is almost no empty space to expand into.  You are often invading by turn two.

No dice involved and not much other luck involved either other than the event cards.

RATING: 7
Lots of things are going on in this game. The game are tough to master at first. The game is long to explain. At first the gameplay felt strange: the game was over after 4 turns (special victory conditions), almost no combat took place and no big units got built.

On the second try, we played more competitvely and the game got much better. I still think it is difficult to get big units on the field: you have to do a lot of research, build, ressouces gathering first but it is a really engaging game. 

I really like the "stack" action mecanism which gives a real dynamism to the "planning phase". The combat with cards is really awesome as its the research "tree" abstracted into a deck.

[Update] I want to like this game. I really do. The bits are superb, the Technology deck + combat system are cool, all the units are really we characterized BUT every game we had has been either bland or boring. The player wait on their CP source, throwing an attack here and there. The game are short and usually won on CP. You don't have enough time to develope your tech tree/buildings or to really use those big units in combat. I think our group plays too conservatively and don't fight enough.

I can't help but think that Nexus Ops bring most of the Starcraft feel while doesn't giving any choices but to fight.

RATING: 6
Risk, upgraded to the modern concept of table-top gaming.
That's it.
Each player gets a faction with its own special rules. Each faction belongs to one of three races, and each race has its own unique units (so two faction from the same race will have tha same units available).
Units are devised in a manner so that they strongly condition the style of play, which is a good thing.
Rules are overly complex, and sometimes clunky, but overall it's a good game, well worth a try.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[b]First impression of the game?[/b]
My impressions are favorable.

[b]What are the highlights?[/b]
Tons of plastic goodness. The order placement system is pretty cool and keeps players engaged. The game does a great job of porting over the system design from the RTS computer game. There is a lot of depth here—a lot to explore. The rules are well-organized and well-written which is a plus with a game this dense.

[b]What are the drawbacks?[/b]
The game is LONG...we didn't make it through an entire game. It took me about an hour to explain the game and we played from 9 p.m. until 2 a.m. In that time, I we played 4 full turns. I think we had probably 2 or 3 more turns to go. I would confirm the comments about confusion regarding the interplay of various units. For example, it's not intuitive, other than brute force, the advantages of a Hydralisk over a Queen.

[b]Who would enjoy this game?[/b]
Hardcore gamers only on this one. Fans of the computer game will definitely enjoy the game and may even have a leg up. This is a full-blown Ameritrash game (without dice).

[b]Who would [i]not[/i] enjoy this game?[/b]
This is not a game you buy for your nephew and try to bang out a session on a Thanksgiving day. It's not light and it's not for casual gamers. But I think that may be obvious...the girth of the box is intimidating enough as it is.

[b]Would you play this game again?[/b]
Yes. It's one of those games, I would love to play again soon...but the reality? It will probably sit on the shelf for another year and I will have to review the rules and the game will take another six hours...*sigh*

RATING: 9
Starcraft is one of my favorite 'long' games.  It's biggest strenght is all the different options and the possible strategies, asymetrical races, beautiful components, and fast and aggressive gameplay.  It's downside is it's lenght (specially with many players) and a steep learning curve thanks to many little rule details that can be easily forgotten, even after repeated play..

I really like the game for 2 players because it's a constant battle over a small territory, and it plays fast.  More experience with 3 players tells me this does not work as well, because if 2 player enter conflict, the third one benefit, and one of the 3 player can be knocked out of the game very quickly.  2 or 4+ are recommended. Zerg vs human or protoss in a 2 player game is also tough to balance due to the zerg ability to hatch more units quicker. In a multiplayer game, a player who is trailing the leaders in points can still bust out a surprise special victory at stage 3, keeping it competitive for everyone.  The rulebook is also really well written with tons of example, pictures, and detailed explanations of each phase, wich is welcome because of all the small details.  It's a good thing because there is a crap load of rules (too many actually).

While this can be a very long game to play, however, the fact that each player resolve one order at a time, means the game stay dynamic and cut the downtime.  I personally don't feel like there is downtime at all with experienced players, everything happens rather quickly.

I used to think Broodwar is a must have, but after a couple of plays,I now believe the base game still is a lot of fun and will provide hours of game play.  BW just add a little more of the same, but the leadership cards are a very nice addition.

Do I recommend this game?  Yes and no.  While I love to play it, I know why some people hate it.  It's just a real chore to learn the rules and require lots of effort. Not knowing the Starcraft universe do not help because a lot of units (specially Protoss) all look the same and it become confusing. Other games in the genre can reach the same goals with less effort.  If you like to fight, you like to push plastic figures and like space theme, however you should check this game.  Like I said, I like it as a 2 player direct conflit, it plays in less than 2 hours.  Maybe I am a masochist, but even if I still scratch my head at some of the rule details after 20 plays, I would play this anytime.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
After 10 plays this game is incredible.  It has all the stuff I like - A theme that fits, a variable setup, too much to learn quickly.  I love playing a game that I have to 'discover' as I go.  For instance, the same race on the same starting planet may have to played totally different since the other planets and races will change.  The components are worth the price of admission - great minis, very durable cards, and big planets that fit everything they need to without being cumbersome.  A minor quibble is the organization of the rulebook is not intuitive (for me) and the index very incomplete (a good start, though!).  I doubt I will tire of this game soon and I know there will be expansions coming to spice it up even more.  This is a 10 in my book.

This is the most addictive boardgame I can recall playing.  Just one more... just one more...  Reminds me of Magic the Gathering without cost too much $$$

And for those FFG haters - the game came with a broken piece that was promptly replaced (so there, nyah!)

My favorite multi-player bash each other game.  I will be playing this in twenty years (if I am around to do so).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.06
Added four more plays. Still trying to decide on the rating. The random factor of setup has a solid chance of screwing somebody on some level. I was eliminated early in one game based on setup, and I won another in vast part due to setup as well. If everybody is of equal skill, the setup really might be the deciding factor.

Another example on the randomness of the game. Player A had the needed 15 VP to win the game. We check out Event III cards and mine says my opponents lose 2 VP. I gain a VP via a leaders card and the game goes one final round. I win since I am now in the lead. I won simply because I happened to draw that one card. If someone wants to buy the game, they really have to accept a certain amount of random factor.

One tip to counter some of it would be to house rule picking three planets and keeping two. If a player, let alone a new player, draws two planets that have crap for resources and they are playing the Protoss, well, their basically screwed. Drawing a third planet at least gives them a chance to get one planet with decent resources.

There is a lot of administration going on in the game. I would have copies of all of the steps the game goes through on a player aid for each player to help counter having that info go through one player. This should also make turns go quicker since people now what they need to work on next between their turn.

Played a three player scenario and I liked it a lot since it's a preset game and players are forced into a semi-coop situation. I actually wished there were more scenarios so those would get played versus the randomly generated game.

How planets are placed can literally force one player to go through another player. Gateways can keep tricky to deal with as well. I guess there is enough randomness it somewhat balances out in the end most of the time.

The uniqueness of the races and their balance is done really well. There really is a lot to like in the game even if it goes beyond how much randomness I want in a game.

Three players games I would deem as being my least favorite. Two and 4-6 players work best. If doing 3 players, play the scenario.

Played with expansion. Playing a friend's copy.

Ratings Potential: 7-8.5. I can see house ruling picking three planets and keeping two as bumping the score. I think an 8 is a realistic peak. Another game that is so close to being awesome and yet...

RATING: 8.4
This game is variously reviewed as the best wargame ever, and the best thematic representation of a computer game in board game format - and these sentiments match up to my experience with this game.

I only brought it to table once, in New Haven with my visiting friend Dylan.  We didn't play it entirely correctly, but we both *loved* it - the best part being a toss-up between the fantastic plastic figurines of classic Starcraft units, or the *EXCELLENT* initiative order determination mechanic (you create a stack of tokens, which both screws with people's carefully laid plans; and also tends to reduce the effectiveness of overwhelming forces if memory serves).

In my recent board game auction here on BGG, I toyed with the notion of selling this mostly-untouched OOP game I paid many pretty pennies to get - and then I realized my foolishness.  This shall continue to sit and await the right group of fellow gamers, and when it again comes to table, it shall be glorious.

DID somebody call an exterminator??

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Rich theme, great strategic choices, nasty combat, well balanced. The learning curve on the rules was steep, and that's really my only complaint.

The more I've played this, the more I am impressed by it... it seems to have an intensity to it as it reaches the climax of the game that few other games have. In my opinion this is due to the PLAY mechanics and not the theme, although the theme is so well integrated into the mechanics that arguably the two cannot be separated.

----------------------

Now that I've played it enough times I can be honest about it's flaws:
1) It's an absolute bitch to teach to new players. Seriously. There is so much going on they they are almost instantly overwhelmed and the best player aids only serve to bog them down and confuse them further.
2) This game defines fiddly. Unnecessary chrome out the nose, and a kajillion little bits and tokens to keep track of the chrome, hyper fragile minis. To a degree this is as much an advantage as a disadvantage, but honestly, when I am playing with teenagers, I am in this constant state of paranoia that some small, very important token is going to be lost in time and space.
3) The game absolutely does not work with six players. It just doesn't. It drags, it takes forever to set up and take down, it gives a heavy advantage to the player who is lucky/skillful enough during planet placement to distance himself from the other five, and you need a table the size of Texas to fit all the bits and pieces on it.

RATING: 7
There is a lot I like about this game.  It is eminently replayable: the game "board" changes every game; each race has their own unique strengths and weaknesses and have a variety of units to build; combat moves along at a brisk pace (I didn't experience the downtime that others have critisized); the technology tree and events add even more variety to the game; the orders system, where players alternate placing chits, is a terrific mechanic that creates a lot of tactical options and rewards forward-thinking; and different paths to victory forces players to think strategically.  The components are awesome and the play time (three hours in my experience) is very short for a game of this scope and size.  Once you get a turn under your belt, the game is intuitive and pretty simple.  Update: Downgraded rating - the game can either be epic or end abruptly before anything gets moving.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
This was so close to being good that it breaks my heart. I recently got the WoW board game, and was really impressed by how good it was, so I thought I'd give Starcraft a try. It is indeed a good game, but I can see why it hasn't been reprinted after all this time.

One thing Starcraft does very well is being an epic game of moving dudes around a map and attacking each other. The rules are incredibly simple despite the thick rulebook. In many ways, the structure of the game reminds me of Star Wars: Rebellion. There is a lot of programming actions followed by carrying them out, but this game utilizes a deck-building mechanism for technology and combat cards. The combat is really simple as well, and this may be the first game that utilizes card combat in a way that I enjoy. Also, the way you use your workers it simple, yet effective. The core engine of this game works really well.

Unfortunately, the game does not feel enough like Starcraft to me.

All the units, races, technologies, etc. are here, but ultimately the game doesn't really translate these well into the game. All the units are very similar, with some technologies helping to differentiate them slightly. Despite the designers trying to capture the theme, it felt like the miniatures were put into this game to sell the game rather than to be utilized as important components. For the most part, units are either ground units, air units, or support units. Beyond that, there are very, very minor differences that don't really differentiate these units from each other.

Along with that, territories are won and lost very quickly. I get that this game is trying to zoom out from a single skirmish to a more abstracted, universal view of what is going on, but it doesn't give me the same feel as playing the PC game. You aren't so much building up bases and chipping away at your opponent as you are expanding on a map and attacking opponents occasionally.

I don't know. It's difficult to put into words why this game doesn't translate the feel of the PC game that well, but it just doesn't for me. The WoW board game really gave me the feel of growing and expanding my character, but this game didn't feel like a game of building a base and sending troops at my opponent. As I mentioned before, Star Wars: Rebellion covers a lot of the same ground, but even more than that, I feel like Nexus Ops captures the feel of playing Starcraft more than this game does. I thought this game was fine, but it's not one I think I'll ever seek out that often.

RATING: 7
Preliminary rating after 1 play (3p, with Brood War).

I like the basic conception and see a couple of cool ideas, mechanisms-wise. Also, 4x is my genre, and miniatures alway add flavour. 

However, the game has 2 significant downsides: 
1.) bad handling. Constant need for checking reference sheets since it's hard to distinguish and to keep track of the various unit types. Confusing, frustrating. ...yet one might say that this is an ambivalent aspect. Having the coice between many different units is actually nice, no doubt.
2.) downtime, last but not least due to my complaint above. The constant need to look up stats results in an unnecessarily long total play time.

And on top of that all, Starcraft really is a beast to learn. The rules are among the heaviest I have seen from FFG so far.

RATING: 9
It's not Ameritrash! (This is due to the fact that it doesn't suck.) 

Starcraft is a great hybrid game, i.e. a game where you fight each other with *shock* PLASTIC(!) miniatures, and is ALSO well designed and balanced. 

The combat is diceless, the mechanics are innovative (order stacks,  battle cards), the game has an economy with limited resources, victory points, catch the leader mechanics...

The only warning I will give is that I enjoy the game more because I have played the computer game. If you are not familiar with the computer game you probably shouldn't buy the board game, although many of the people I have played it with have still enjoyed the game without experience of the computer game.

RATING: 6
For a big game, it's pretty approachable, with only some of the combat twists requiring minutae to be studied. The game is slower than I expected from the rules explanation, with people having to place orders in reverse and react to other people's play. And the combat I expected faster as well with only one card to play per skirmish and it's resolved, but it takes time to find the best cards and weigh against expected defense. To play well you also need to study your technology deck and choose a complementary building strategy, which takes more time. So obviously the game goes faster with experience and study, but I was hoping and expecting faster from the get-go. But at least it delivers on the promise of strategic choice combined with tactical game play and I can see the rating rising with repeat play.

RATING: 9
Bought this game at 1/3 it's normal price! The box was somewhat damaged but other than that, no big deal. Components in perfect condition (apart of the stands of flying units, but well...) and game looks gorgeous!

Another go in this game it is settled. This is the type of game you want to play with regulars. Buddies who commit to making an effort in knowing this game really well. Whenever a new player is brought into it, it takes painstakingly long! I believe it should be fairly reasonable to bring playing time down to 2,5 - 3 hours with 4 players. But it needs to be insisted on. So this is definitely not the type of game you want to introduce to casual players... stick to Stone Age instead... or Bohnanza! :)

Major complaint - the cardboard pieces, although they look nice, are really bad quality! Since the navigation routes won't fit properly into the planets, they easily start to tear and wear, so I won't even bother to attach them, which takes away some of its gorgeous looks! That's what happens when games are made in China! No getting around it and FFG just won't stop selling worse quality for the same price! That really bothers me!

- Rules are exhausting! 40 pages of little details. But the book is well structured and there are lots of tips. A good work.
