=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Good, tight game. There are several layers of interacting strategy. The stacking of the orders allows a lot of player screwage both through blocking and bluffing. Complex card combat system that takes some getting used to, but it is much better than just rolling dice because you have some control over what you are doing. Overall, a tight, tense game that makes you think on multiple levels and is all about player interaction. The small maps and the objectives force players to interact almost immediately and throughout. Great adaptation of the video game which I love as well. It feels similar to an RTS because the pace is quick, and you always have to be thinking ahead but also willing to adapt on the fly depending on what your opponents do. There are several strategies available for each faction, and the components are excellent. Works well with up to three players; with 4 or 6, I would suggest 2v2 or 3v3 matches, like the video game (you will obviously need to alter the victory conditions, but this makes the game deliciously complex). Almost all of the negative comments on here are from people who played once and couldn't understand the rules. This takes more commitment than that to fully enjoy. It probably won't start shining until about your third play when you start to put everything together.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Very, very cool. I was skeptical when the design notes talked about how cards work like hit points, and you shuffle in technologies. But it all works really well. Any element of the game can be decisive.

I now mostly play with a simplifying variant (and with Brood War): No modules (except supply), special-victory leadership cards only (no II or III), predetermined board geometry with random planets, no resource depletion or tokens, no guard or defend, certain event cards related to the above removed.

This keeps the core of the game (card play and order play) but removes numerous special rules.

RATING: 7
Lots of depth and strategy with very little randomness. It's a very well designed game that allows for lots of variety from play to play. The downside, however, is the playing time, which is around one hour per player in most cases. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Awesome simulation of the RTS style of computer game. Reminds me of Nexus Ops in several ways, but with far more depth, variety and replayability (due to the more complex rules and the different races available). Also has way more components! Definitely a game that takes a good number of plays to properly explore.

The abstraction to a larger planet-based scale works well (as opposed to what could have been more of a skirmish-based wargame).

The way the order tokens are placed & executed keeps people involved throughout, rather than waiting while all the other players perform their turns separately.

The combat system is perhaps a little convoluted but does introduce some unpredictability without resorting to dice. It will take a long time to learn all the nuances of each race's units & cards. The special abilities on cards and the technology cards that can be purchased and added to your own personal combat deck lend it some of the feel of a CCG.

Because the board scales (2 planets per player) this game genuinely plays well with the whole range of player numbers.

The rules are definitely longer than was needed and they repeat themselves on multiple occasions. Still, this is better than having unclear rules. Unfortunately there are a few errors on the cards, summary sheets & the back page of manual - really should have been checked more thoroughly before release, but its all covered in the official FAQ.

Good integration of theme & excellent components - except for some bent figures and the well-known issues with broken stands on the flying units (I had 15!!!) But those are easily glued back together & figures can be straightened with a quick blast from a hair-dryer.

Huge box - even with this amount of plastic to store, it is still too big!

NOTE: Loudest & most realistic box fart I've experienced so far!

RATING: 8
12-10-2014. Rated after 1 game.
Starcraft: The boardgame is an older FFG title (OMG, I love FFG) I was recently able to pick up a while ago on the Dutch equivalent of Craigslist. 

The game is a Twilight Imperium 3 light-ish game, in which you 'Explore' (well.. not really), 'Expand' (yes!),'Exterminate' (surely) and 'Exploit' (literally). In this game, you win by eliminating your opponents, get 16 VPs or get your special objective. 

The game is played over rounds. Each turn, you can place 4 actions on the board. You can move onto a planet or move your troops on your own planet with move, you can take a build action to build buildings/research and research to upgrade your/get new units / buy combat cards or new technologies. You have 2 tokens of each. The top counter is resolved first, but all tokens on each planet are stacked on top. So if your opponents move token is on top of your build token.. he can attack first before you can build defenses. You can also forego an action to get a special event card that is resolved at the end of the round.

Combat is resolved in an interesting way. Basically, the attacker lines up the troops and assigns which unit attacks which. Excess units are put into support units. Then each player plays a combat card supplemented with upgrade card if able. Then, you look at your combat card and see if the units on the combat card match the frontline unit, if this is the case you use the outer and often best set of attack and health numbers. If it does not match, you use the inner mostly less impressive ones. You then add support bonus to see what your combat strength is. If you beat your opponent and you can target him legally he removes his unit from the board. (ie. if your opponent has a flying unit and your unit cannot target flying units, you do not inflict damage). You do the same if your opponents combat strength exceeds your units. If at the end of combat at least 1 defending unit remains, you've lost and need to retreat.

After resolving all actions, the turn ends. Victory conditions are checked and players may look at their event cards and play 1. 

Opinion:
I love Starcraft. I've played with 3, which made for the fact that each faction from the starcraft universe was represented. I got completely killed, but had a great time being killed. Game is streamlined, plays relatively quickly and gives you a nice 4x feel. Great game, want to play more.

RATING: 6
Huge sprawling space battle...   kinda...  but much more than that.   I prefer this by far to Twighlight Imperium 3, and next to Tide of Iron this is my favorite of the large FFG games.   Though I havn't played Starcraft the video game, It definately feels right in terms of how you build in this one.  There are many decisions to be made and a lot of planning, yet all of it is truncated to the essentials...  very efficient game play!  While not all of your actions are exciting, I disagree with those that say there is a lot fo down time.  In one 5 player game, there were 100  actions taken in just over 3 hours.   Having said that, this is a Conquest game and as such requires a certain type of gamer.  With the individual faction win conditions the game is quite managable for time sake and after an initial explanation, this is very easy to pick up for new players...  while having a lot of depth for returning players.

Update:  As of a second play I think there are multiple learning curves for this game...  learn the basic...   learn the races...  learn how to stop the Zerg.  This has lead me to believe that the Zerg special win conditions are a bit easier to accomplish than the other 4 factions, but only more time with the game will tell.

Game Play: :star: :star: :star: :star: :nostar:
Replayability: :star: :star: :star: :halfstar: :nostar:
Design/Art/Bits: :star: :star: :halfstar: :nostar: :nostar:

RATING: 6
OK lemme talk about Starcraft.
The whole game is about getting to the victory conditions, which are normally getting a certain number of victory points. You get these by controlling planetary areas which grant Victory points at the end of each turn. First person to 15 points wins. Otherwise you can get your factions special victory condition, which could be having a base on 3 different planets, controlling every area on 2 different planets, controlling 6 different victory areas etc.
Its all about holding planetary areas. To do this you need to be able have troops there that can beat off anybody that tries to attack you and knock you out of those areas. So the game consists of building up the right types of troops and fighting with them. If your main opponent is going for marines and tanks then you want to bring flying units at him because then he can't shoot them effectively. Thats pretty much all the game is about. Its like Axis & Allies. There is no diplomacy and the strategies aren't deep at all. You have little skirmish fights and you'll win or lose, but they never get that exciting really. Then you get to the Stage III part of the game due to the event deck and someone has won the game and it all feels pretty unclimactic.
The good parts are the figures, the planning phase, and I guess the combat system is pretty good.
I spent any downtime in the game having little fights with the figures. I borrowed my neighbours unused Zerg monsters and had a little shootout with sound effects and everything. It was *epic*. More enjoyable than actually playing the boardgame, but my other neighbour said it was childish and distracting. I said he was anally retentive then invaded his home planet and blew his base up in the game and he threw a big sulk, so HA! to him :).
As you know from reading the rules, In the planning phase you put down 4 orders on different planets one at a time. When you're scrapping over a small cluster of planets you're blocking and covering up your opponent's orders with your own, so you can mobilise in to his areas before he can build reinforcements and stuff like that. Its quite tactical getting your orders in the right order, and trying to guess what he is up to. You get a huge advantage in going after your opponent, so seating order is important. We played 4 player, and I was at war with the guy to my right. That put him at a huge advantage, as I got to place the last order on him 3/4 of the time (other 1/4 time I was first player). Its a good system, but its not *that* great. Other games do the bluff and outwitting thing a lot better.
Finally, the combat system is pretty good. I like the way they've managed to put the all the different units capabilities on to the base combat cards, with more special effects being put in to the deck via technology purchases. It *can* be quick, but a lot of the time the other players were taking *ages* picking their cards for the skirmishes, and this caused a lot of downtime.
Lets start summarising. I was hoping for the ultimate 2-6 player game, that I could have a quick skirmish with with a few, and it turns in to an epic war with more.
I think it failed for me on both counts, mainly because I want more diplomacy and "breadth of gameplay". My favourite games like Struggle of Empires and Mare Nostrum have lots of things going on. Starcraft doesn't have any gameplay breadth because its all about building the right units, getting the tech cards that match them in to your combat deck, and shooting the enemy. Because of the lack of any other form of interaction though, experienced players should be able to rip through games of this *really* quickly. I could see a 2-3 player game being over in an hour. I think 5-6 players isn't worth it because it adds on downtime, and you never get to interact with the other players, you'll still only have 2-3 neighbours.
But I'd personally play something like Bonaparte at Marengo because there is much more cerebral challenge, much more depth of strategy and tactics. Or I'd play something more interesting and thematic like Marvel Heroes. And at the higher player end I'll play something with more breadth.
After putting all my thoughts together and writing this mini-review for you though, I've noticed that I had kind of written the game off after my first real play. I sat down in the best of mindsets for playing it, humming the Starcraft and Battlezone music to myself, having little shootouts with the figures, saying "Lets go Marines!" everytime I did a mobilise (my right neighbour really appreciated all this ;)). But the game still left my a bit bored. I'd definitely play it again, but its really plumetted down my wishlist from first place to down to the bottom. Its still *on* the list though, because I think with 2-4 experienced players, when you're in the mood for a fun simple shootout its a great fit, so don't let me discourage you too much. I guess the easiest way to explain it is that its quite a few tactical step-ups from Nexus Ops.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
This game looks like a unsuccessful try to improve [i][url=http://www.juegosdemesacr.com/cms/index.php?option=com_content&task=view&id=347&Itemid=1]Game of Thrones[/url][/i], that achieves its objective in components (the miniatures are the best ones that I've ever seen), but not in game mechanics. The main problem with [i]Game of Thrones[/i] was the unbalanced map, and this is fixed in a clever way with a modular board. They also took away the support command, which added some difficulty; worked harder to give each faction a special feeling, even with special victory conditions for each of them, limited the number of orders available per turn, and allowed multiple orders in the same space, which is the main advance in the game and force players to think backwards. Nevertheless, some interesting features are also gone, like the Iron Throne auctions and the event cards, which now play a different role and have individual effects and not global ones. Overall, [i]Game of Thrones[/i] is still a better game, but this can be pulled from the shelves from time to time for a change.

RATING: 10
I really want to love this game, with the nifty order-stacking resolution system, the novel deck-management approach to technology and combat, and soooo many unique sculpts + stands for flying units. But the sheer number of technologies and unit types (9 to 11) bogs the game down. Excellent homage for video-game fans, but the swath of units and technologies is harder to justify clunking the game up for me and necessitating more homework.

I'd love it if I had more time to explore this game. The originality and painstaking attention to video-game detail is commendable.

Screw it: I'd play this some more.

[hr]

Raised from 7 to 8. It's a blast to play (unit/tech complexity concern was overblown and allows for varied repeat plays), since you're fighting for VP spots immediately and ruthlessly, and only tech/upgrade when there happens to be leeway. For now, some unintuitive rule interactions create a lot of disappointments with sub-optimal plays. Hoping to iron this out and get more to the mind games, cunning plays and risk-taking (e.g. planning for a gold order you don't have yet, ensuring your Orbital Defense is out in time). 

Hoping at a high/expert level, the order-placement won't boil down to a couple of scripted plays in various situations (first-seat mode, last-seat mode, prevent-invasion mode etc).

All-out war from the get-go, with attackers getting good bonuses. Unlike with other games, there's no 'warm-up' or 'exploration' or any other get-to-know-you phase to ease into the conflict, just balls to the wall, and squeezing in research while you're busy replacing your fallen troops. Not for the faint of heart.

[hr]

Lowered design rating from 5 to 4, because there are too many unintuitive technicalities and 'glitches'. They provide a differentiator for the expert (e.g. Twilight Struggle), but I think it's overkill here, with all the complexity elsewhere. It's inexcusable that my plays can still be decided on technicalities and gotcha-moments. 

Glitches include destroying own stuff any time on turn (enabling pseudo-Liftoff base-replacement, weird liberation rules in team-play), Weasley Retrograde, the entire Defensive Support Module, force-mining to save workers, empty-base glitches (better to leave base empty than defend so attacker doesn't draw 5 cards on a Gold Mobilize), the bad rules on moving into an undefended enemy base (Runewars, Corey's later and more mature effort, handles it much better by calling it and interpreting it consistently as a battle), weird interactions with non-matching attack types + support units + splash damage + cloaking + sacrifice to enable weird-ass unit exchanges). The base game also messed up the Marine (splash damage!), made Archons too powerful (unproven, but in magicgeek I trust), and provided an orbital defense exploit in team-play. Too many oversights.

5+5

RATING: 7
[1 play] 5 players, 1 hour of rules explanation, 4 turns complete, 6 hours total, and probably 2-3 more hours until completion. Did I have fun? Of course. The game has some cool things going for it and I love the dynamics of our gaming group. Did I have 6 hours worth of equivalent gaming fun? Probably not, but that isn't necessarily a fair analysis given that it was the trial run of an admittedly epic game.

What I liked: The stacking of orders mechanic is great. The map of the planets and the "3-D" connections system between worlds is innovative. The bits are awesome and plentiful - if a bit bewildering in their variety.

What I didn't embrace: The learning curve is significant both in the initial learning of rules (and we are not at all inexperienced gamers in all styles of games), and what I expect will be a continued  steep learning curve in future games figuring out how to play well with the development trees and the interactions of units in battle.

What I didn't like: The pure length of the game. I am finding that while I enjoy wargames of significant length when played 2 player, I have less tolerance for monster games with lots of people than I used to. Each additional player adds downtime, chaos, and kingmaking potential that I find less and less enthralling when games start crossing the 3 or 4 hour mark with absolutely no end in sight. If it doesn't have a very realistic chance of wrapping up in under 4 hours, then multiplayer may not be for me. 

To sum up my thoughts, I'd enjoy playing this again, but I think I'd really prefer 3 players. (Maybe 4 players max.) 3 players would be an outstanding way to balance the bash the leader impetus and also bring gametime down to a realistic 3 or 3.5 hours.

Most recent play 9-11-09 at Garrett's with Michael, Carter, Joel, and Garrett.

RATING: 9
This game is practically built for me.

Fierce, fast (pacing wise, game feels fast paced even though the game length is long) highly competitive and asymmetric just like its source of inspiration.

Dozens of thematic flavor rules and units/techs to memorize give this game a seemingly steep barrier to entry to those who have not played the computer game (in reality it's not nearly as bad as it seems). But for those who have, and are into this type of board game, this is a real treat.

Still, don't expect it to capture the computer game completely in feel. This is a board game set in the Starcraft universe that does a great job with the source material, but it's not really a 1:1 translation of the computer game. There's no direct representation of hidden tech, no scouting, no positioning, etc, which are defining features of a computer RTS game.

I will point out though that many of those elements are abstracted in a fun way -- Leadership cards (Brood War)  act like a build-order of sorts, with their variable unit and worker counts and double-blind simultaneous selection. Position your two Zealots at a "choke point" (Low unit limit area) and they can hold off a swarm of Zerglings for far longer than if you have them in the open field (high unit count area)

I've seen people say that games like Nexus Ops give the Starcraft feel better because of its fast-paced skirmish feel. This is laughable and a confession that you never played Starcraft -- asymmetry is front and center in Starcraft, so Nexus Ops's generic identical factions hold no candle.

I'm glad it does its own thing so well though instead of slavishly trying to adapt the computer game. Just happy that it even exists, and the valuable Starcraft license wasn't abused to make some crappy mass market game instead.

Finally, this is a skillful game, with all randomness and imperfect information being EXTREMELY manageable, from the hidden orders other players place to the cards you draw. You will feel in control of your destiny here.

The Brood:War Expansion is really, really, really, good -- this is one game where there is really no excuse not to have the expansion. It's just wasted potential without it.

[b]2 Player:[/b] Amazing competitive fast paced 2 player game. Probably my favorite way to play.

[b]3 Player:[/b] Great count for an FFA!

[b]4 Player:[/b] 2v2 works great in this game.

[b]5-6 player[/b]: Haven't tried yet. I imagine that they will be epic and the game length will be eternal. This style of game probably better left to Twilight Imperium; Starcraft is more focused on surgical strikes and paying attention to everything your opponent(s) are doing, so having too many opponents I imagine will detract from that.

Base Game: (5 Design + 4 Enjoyment)
w/ Expansion: (5 Design + 5 Enjoyment)

RATING: 4
The bits are awesome, the action stacking is clever and the tech growth is really engaging.  There's a whole lot of color here and for a while the game is quite enjoyable.  Where the game fails for me is that while the combat is nicely detailed, ultimately you spend a whole lot of time and energy tailoring your attack/defensive positions with your cards, min/maxing everything, when ultimately it ends up being just about as random as rolling dice (Yes, I know there is some hand management and card seeding from research).  To make matters worse you kind of have to manage the battles this way as the margins are so slim, you just aren't afforded the luxury to take this lightly.  This combat system makes me long for a quick throw of the dice and a CRT.  It's just so much work, for such uninteresting results, matching numbers, see who wins. Ugh, exhausting.

Throughout the game I kept feeling like this was constantly operating at 5 steps forward and 4 steps back, unless you weren't a target that turn in which case, the sheer level of upkeep for everything may have paid off.

I know that much of the visual design was taken from the video game, but I just found much of it confusing.  You are expected to know the names of all the units, you have no idea what the capabilities of these mysteriously named units are (Can this enemy's crazy unit attack air units?).  And the Icons are just unintuitive and/or busy.  The Build looks like some mobile lunar unit, and the Research looks like a futuristic building.  I know that you get used to this, but even still, the player aid is so busy that I have to get right up close to see what is going on on anyone's mat.  The units are also a bit confusing especially with the Marines and the Firebats. Even controlling the humans I had a hard time trying to figure them out.  And with so much clutter on the table, why not create a battle board so that battles can be clearly lined up and cards lain out accordingly.

I'm also not a fan of the end game.  It's pretty obvious that some players are unlikely to win at a certain point as everyone encroaches their victory conditions (especially in a 6 player game) so players are constantly forced to try to prevent a winner.  Because of the layout of the board, there are often times where players that can't win will be deemed responsible to hold back the leader.  It seems like this game will frequently end in a kingmaker situation.  For the player that is stuck biding time trying to slow the lead players I liken it to getting terminal cancer.  If you get the chemotherapy it can extend your life eight more months, but they will all be miserable vs letting the disease take hold, having one good month and one painful month then death. 

With six the game drags massively and the level of interaction is based solely on who can be reached, and ends up feeling like the most complicated game of Bang produced.  As one person put it, punch your neighbor in the shoulder and hope that he punches the next guy.  

I just can't see playing this when Nexus Ops just does it significantly better.  This feels so flaccid in comparison, and it's hard for me not to compare the two.

RATING: 8
I'm a huge fan of Starcraft (the PC game) as well as Starcraft 2.  Just seeing this in board game format is a dream come true.  It's rated so high because of this fact, coupled with the fact that it's actually executed well.  Not only as a stand alone board game, but the translation from PC to board game is really great!

Most concepts from the computer game translate well into the board game.  Do you get a lot of lower tier units out as fast as possible for map control and a rush strategy? Or do you turtle up, defend your base, and tech up to higher tier units?  If you can think of it in the computer game, you can do it in the board game! And it's viable.

The rulebook makes learning the game a much bigger chore than it should be considering the game isn't really that complicated in the end. Other than that the only downside to this game is that the whole concept and theme of the game just doesn't appeal to some people I normally play board games with so I'm not sure how often I'll get to play it. RATING: 7
[FFG 07 edition] {Fantasy war, computer game; tile-laying, resource-management, battle cards, variable-winning-goal; optimal 2p or 2v2} {Normal: 7; Galactic Conquest Variant: 6; Team play variant: 7.5} StarCraft is a nice galactic war game.  Although the rulebook is 48 pages long, it's easy to understand the system and mechanics. But players have to remember some fiddly rules.  I love the stack of order system - it requires players to plan in advance what they should do, not just react to what happens.  The battle system is quite nice - both attackers and defenders have certain advantages, thought it takes time to resolve a single battle (rolling dice is faster, but I don't care).  The victory conditions are very obvious. It's easy to notice what player is winning. Resources are very tight and it's difficult to advance every military unit or technology. Players have to adapt their growth path smartly.  Player interaction is very strong.

The components are nicely made and informative. The miniatures are colorful, nicely molded and the plastic is soft enough to avoid breaking.  However, it's bad that the reference sheet has an error and the order reference of regrouping phase at the end of the rulebook is wrong.

RATING: 6
What I really liked: The hidden movement chits were inspired. What a great idea. It requires you to think backwards, but it really gives you great power to mess with the turn sequence and initiative. I also liked the way the new technologies were shuffled into your deck instead of being given to you outright.

What I kinda liked: You begin the game in close quarters, so the fighting starts almost immediately. Plus, attackers have a serious advantage.

What was sort of a drag: Too many bits, cards, chits to juggle in my player area. Plus, the fighting part itself wasn't that much fun. Too much information on the cards. Lotsa numbers, lotsa text, lotsa icons. Yuck. Here's where they could have borrowed some sugar from Nexus Ops, where combat is short, sweet and simple.

Overall, I think this may be a game that I'll use to scratch the space game itch more often than Twilight Imperium 3. TI:3 still rules, imo, but it's a heavyweight, and Starcraft is a middleweight. Pound-for-pound, Starcraft may be the better game.

RATING: 6
Gorgeous components. Its neat that there's a lot to do, with numerous technologies and unit combinations to try out.
Way too much 'stuff', with almost a dozen assorted piles of cards in the game, and too many mechanics that don't interact well going on at once.  Individually some of them are clever; like shuffling the technology cards into your deck.  But the combination is very clunky. There can be a lot of snowball effect, and the randomness of the event cards can be annoyingly swingy. 
Seems difficult to play as a free for all; the sides which avoid combat initially will have a large advantage. Combat is highly detrimental to both sides. 

House rule:
When a battle starts, assign frontline and support units as normal, but do not decide on skirmishes. The attacker and defender each take turns choosing one of their frontline units to attack one frontline unit of the opponent. This skirmish proceeds normally. Each frontline unit may only be chosen as an attacker once per combat, but may be targeted with multiple attacks. Conduct a number of skirmishes equal to the number of frontline units on one side. If a side has no frontline units left, their turn to choose passes and the opponent gets the choice instead.

RATING: 7
Let me just start by saying the game loses an automatic point from me for having 7 broken flying unit bases out of the box.  It would lose two for poorly produced components except they did a fucking marvelous job on the units themselves.  Game wise its a brilliant adaptation of the computer game.  

Off the bat I thought the 3D space mechanics were quite inspired and brilliant.  Even the combat system was well thought out and fairly easy to learn, I love the playing cards out of class and still having a value etc.  Card wise I do feel having 2 face down decks and a hand of cards deck sized laid down leaves room for critical error, especially if you drink and game like I do, its far to easy to pick up the wrong deck instead of your hand, infact this did happen to me.  The game is quite long lasting about 3 hours, it was fairly engaging however, the token placing round system makes things FEEL fairly fast-paced.  The tokens turn orders help make the turns remain interesting and aid in wait times. The basic races are all unique but they do feel a little bland, only the objective comes into play where as the Videogames unique qualities do not, IE sacrificing drones to build buildings, warping in Protoss structures or using more SCVs to build, their all the same at their core.  All and all the special mission objectives won the game for those whom did not engage in the actual major conflict at hand.  I was winning the entire game battling it out against my Protoss enemies, I had a massive lead and was very close to completing my objective.  It seems as if because the other two players turtled they won the game, I'm really not sure how I feel about that since war should be at the heart of the game.

Addendum: after playing twilight imperium, I bitched about a 3 hour playtime on this one, shame on me.

RATING: 5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Overall, I think it does a good job of capturing the feel of the computer game. The action chip system really makes the game interesting instead of a simple slugfest that it could devolve in to. It creates a layer in the game of planning out your movements in such a way that you can tie up another player's actions to allow you to build and reinforce your areas before your opponent is allowed to attack you. This adds strategy and tactics to the game and is one of the things that would hold my interest longer that a simpler game would (Risk 2210, Nexus Ops). Also, the research and upgrading fit in well. It doesn't cost a lot to upgrade your buildings, but with limited resources each turn and a small amount of starting units it isn't cheap enough that you would do all research at the  beginning of the game. The game paces it right so that your techs and upgrades aren't all built by the end of the game, this gives some variety to the different games you'll play. It's worth the extended time the game takes.(learning game with 4 players - 3.5 hours)

RATING: 5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I appreciate StarCraft more each time I play it.  An innovative design that encourages strong tactical play over long-term strategery.

It reminds me a lot of A Game of Thrones.  The evolved take on the hidden order system is the most obvious link between the two.  Both can also be very unforgiving to minor mistakes, and this game isn't very intuitive so newbie mistakes are to be expected.

One thing I don't like is the wildly imbalanced event cards, though this seems to be less of a problem the more players that are in the game.

It's also disappointing that the games don't seem to have much narrative as I loved the story of the video game's campaign.  But the great figures don't let me forget that I'm definitely playing StarCraft.  (Brood War's scenarios may add the narrative I was missing.)

UPDATE: Played again after a long hiatus.  Raised my rating from a 7.5 to a 9.  Awesome tactical combat game.  Why the hell did I care that there was no narrative?  This isn't capturing the campaign, it's capturing BattleNet.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
This is a marvelous adaptation of the computer game. Even the combat system - which I was afraid to be eceedingly complex like the one in Age of Mythology - suits the game just fine. Despite being a long game (>3 hours), it feels like a fast-paced game, because there is so much action right on from the start. My only concern are the flyer units, they seem to be really fragile. I am happy it doesn't use any real-time mechanics, yet in the way players' orders are resolved you get some of that real-time feeling. I now know what it is that I am missing in this game. It is the uniqueness of each factions' workers. There's no game mechanics that involve warping from the Protoss, Brooding by the Zerg and building and repairing by the Terrans. The workers merely exist to harvest resources. Still, this is a great game. 
--------------------------------
Update: I think I'm through with this game. There's just too much conflict going on. And while I like the card-driven mechanic of combats after two sessions in which I got attacked by everybody ( in a 4- and a 3-player game) I'd almost wish to go back to a pure luck-driven combat. You can hold on to fighting two opponents at the same time for a while but not long if you are the only one that has to cope with two or more opponents. Plus, even though you can draw some satisfaction out of fighting off multiple opponents you cannot improve on your developments or armies as you would want and can do if only having to cope with one enemy commander.And in the end you WILL be overrun, since in the time you were busy fighting off opponents,trying to rebuild your armies,your opponents invested in developments you couldn't invest in, because rebuilding armies is quite expensive itself. Eventually your opponent comes with armytypes and technologies you have nothing to subtend to. 
----------------------------
Yet another session in. And once again it showed that it's not worthwhile with three players. If this were three-player only I'd rate it a 3. The game can however be better with more and even better wih an even amount of players. Hence the 5.5
----------------------------
After the somewhat unfortunate 3player-constellations I have a very strong urge to play this game again with more players involved and with the expansion.

RATING: 7.5
A very true-to-form translation of the source material.  This has positives and negatives for me.  Positives: LOTS of options for building, research, etc;  Carefully-balanced and very thematic asymmetry; Interesting special victory conditions.  Negatives:  First, Starcraft's absolute disregard for any sense of scale has always seemed sort of strange to me--a battle cruiser just seems like it should be...huger.  In board game terms, it means that the base units just don't seem that much less powerful than the ones at the top of the tech tree.  Second, RTS games focus so heavily on combat that any secondary objectives, irrespective of how important they are to one's ultimate victory, seem always less important than just FIGHTING.  That's not a disadvantage itself, but for me it becomes so when combined with the sense of scale, because it means that players are forced into immediate conflict with one another, and you have to gear up your resources with as much alacrity as possible.  Now, this says more about me than it does an RTS game, but I don't WANT to gear up my resources quickly.  I want to max out all of my tech trees and roll over the opposition with a huge force; there's just not the time to do that in a multiplayer RTS game.  They nailed the computer game experience faithfully enough that I feel the same playing this: too much to do, too little time.  That is a perfectly good board game maxim, but it's one of the things that drives me crazy about RTS games.  So I'm split about this.  Will gladly play it again, and the "system" aspect of these big box games means I'd likely be able to find the perfect balance for my taste if I tinkered with it enough (But somewhere in my brain I'll fantasize about playing a 2P game on an 8-planet board...).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
A good game with lots of moving parts. An enormity of cards, special powers, different units (beautiful miniatures), some light deck-building, and others things are merged in this game.

It has a good deal of interaction, sice there is, usually, dispute for the control of regions. The combat is a really neat part of the game: the units stay in a line, and one at a time they face each other (but some units, with the use of some cards, can affect the units behind the one engaged) - it is in this moment that the choice of cards are important, since you draw then for the combat.

A solid game from the Fantaty Flight machine, but one that, now, is hard to get.

RATING: 5
After one play with four players, I have to conclude that Starcraft is an OK tactical game with some unwieldy components and clunky rules. To sum it up, it is allright but unremarkable. Sure you get a lot of bits for your money, but the usability of some of the counters and boards is questionable at best. Neither are the miniatures of a high standard. My biggest complaint with this game, however, is that I would rather play something more strategic (like Twilight Imperium) if I have to go over such a large amount of rules and have to put with a playing time of several hours. Starcraft may have a reasonable price/bits ratio, but the price is way too high for the amount of fun you get out of it. If your are looking for something similar, buy Dust instead.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
A combination of a lot of successful mechanics from other games, Starcraft isn't anything new, and that's it's biggest failing.  Many games, most notably [gameid=12493] and [gameid=72125] cover this type of theme and bits, the order system is very similar to [gameid=6472] or [gameid=22038] with a procedural twist, and the unit upgrade system is directly ripped from [gameid=7479].  The one system that's not derivative is the combat cards and skirmish system, which is unintuitive, awkward, and overly specific.  There's an awful lot of deck- and hand-management in a game which is supposed to be about tactics and resources.  The cards do  enforce the need for mixed forces, but introduce an unsavory element of I have a card/I don't have a card.  

This game is clearly for fans of the video game.  There's tons of non-intuitive chrome that arises from slavish devotion to the computer game, but is not summarized well anywhere in the components.  All of the people in my group who are big fans of the computer game can see past this, and everybody who is unfamiliar with the series or who is only a casual fan finds the skirmish system frustratingly unintuitive.

There's no need to track this down, especially for the prices it's going for now.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: N/A
Three powerful alien races battle for control of the galaxy. Whether you choose to lead the versatile Terran, mysterious Protoss, or voracious Zerg, in StarCraft: The Board Game you'll command an army like no other in the universe!
\13\\13\
Once again, Fantasy Flight Games brings one of the world's best-loved computer games to your tabletop. Players take control of the Protoss, Terran, or Zerg and battle across multiple worlds. True to the StarCraft legacy, each of the three races features a unique and distinctive play style, and the inclusion of two distinct factions for each race allows up to six players to compete for galactic dominance at a time.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
As usual, Corey Konieczka has done a terrific job of translating a popular licensed I.P. into an excellent boardgame. Like Twilight Imperium, this epic game comes with loads of cards, giant piles of awesome plastic miniatures and a ridiculous amount of cardboard. The game plays fairly quickly (once you have the hang of it) and all of the factions and units behave exactly as they should in order to be faithful to the StarCraft universe. The card based combat took a little getting used to, but eventually it flows together rather well. The only difficult thing about the StarCraft boardgame is the way it handles player actions. Everyone places an order token face down and if anyone else plays an order in the same zone they are stacked on top of one another. They are then resolved in order from the top down. Don't get me wrong, this is a really cool system, as it succeeds in giving the whole game a "real time" flavor with all the actions piling up and players trying to react to them...but it does require a ton of concentration. If you accidentally stack your command tokens in the wrong order, there is a very strong possibility that you will not be able to do anything and effectively lose a turn. I've had it happen to me and boy does it suck!

It takes a while to learn the factions and their unique strengths and weaknesses, but once you do the game gets even better. If nothing else, Fantasy Flight did an excellent job of capturing the asymmetrical nature of the StarCraft races. This is a very faithful translation. If you're a Blizzard fan, you owe it to yourself to pick this up.

Update: I am coming back to this entry a few years later and I'd like to point out that Corey Konieczka sort of invented the deck building genre in this game. Combat is resolved through draws from a deck of cards unique to each faction and the deck is improved from turn to turn by adding more powerful cards to the deck after your faction has finished researching the technology that awards them!

That being said, that is the ONLY thing I don't like. Even the planet setup is engrossing. I love the stack system, the tech tree is inspired, and it does a phenomenal job of capturing the spirit of an RTS in an extremely playable board game. This is worth the price of admission to me.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
There's so much you WANT to like here.  Cool units, neat ideas, tech trees, interesting theme.

What you get is an overcomplicated mess of a game that clearly underlines the importance of playtesting (or lack thereof). The ruleset is just horrid.  It is overly ambigous and filled with contradictions and exceptions.  Add to that the 60 page rulebook, and you'll find every game, every turn, you'll forget one rule or another.  It's massively complicated. Every rule has another rule that changes the last rule.  The word 'except' and 'however' appear 40 times in the standard rulebook alone.  Add the expansion for more exceptions.

End result is that you dont WIN or LOSE a battle (which is what this game is supposed to be about) due to superior strategy, strength or power, but 8 of 10 times, from another person exploiting a rule you'd forgotten about.  End result frustration, arguing.. eh, yea... fun.

The extremely long playtime and huge downtime add to the pain.  I once played 6 player game at a con with 4 newbies.  We finally quit after 9 hours without declaring a winner. It was common to have 45 minutes to an hour before it got around to your turn again.

The tragic thing is, there's so much potential here that you want to slug through it all and find that buried treasure.  Alas, after a half-dozen plays, I'm conceding that if there's treasure in there, it's bured so deep you'd have to demolish the whole block to get to it.

It need's a eurogamer's touch to completely re-write this thing.  Starcraft's designers seemed to fix rule problems by adding more rules.  Which creates a never ending spiral -> always adding one more rule to fix the last bad one.
